Secret files strip Hughes of his mystery 

WASHINGTON - The secret files of the late Howard 
Hughes strip him of the mystery he used to hide behind. 

Vet these fascinating tiles, now in tile custody of the Internal Revenue 
Service, raise some startling new questions. 
The improbable Hughes was deeply involved with the Central Intelligence 
Agency in some of Ms darkest opera­ tions. including the attempts on the life of Cuba’s Fidel ( astro 
Hughes' man iii Washington. Robert 
Bennett, also worked closely with the 
Watergate plotters. 
Vet the files portray Hughes as a pathetic, if eccentric, old man who slowly wasted away — a dope addict, of uncertain sanity, xx ho measured time by enemas. 
A staff memo. for instance, called 
Ins attention to a statement lie had made “ several enemas ago.” 
Confined self 
He spent his last years confined in an asylum of his own creation, alternately making inuit i in ill ion-dolla r decisions and issuing elaborate instructions on the disposal cf his penthouse waste. 
Once, he directed that it be removed in a “ taped brown sack” and “ dumped into some refuse container” far re­ moved from the hotel. 
In the end. he died of sheer neglect although he was surrounded by doctors and servants in a luxurious Acapulco hotel. 
The last doctor who examined 
Hughes reported to the Mexican auth­ orities that “ it wasn’t a matter of a fatal illness but a patient who'had been neglected." The local medics “ could have saved him." attested the doctor. 
Our curiosity was aroused by the billionaire’s mysterious midnight de­ parture from Las Vegas on Thanks­ giving eve, 197b. 
We picked up whispers that there was something dreadfully w rong xx itll 
Hughes. By Dee. 9, 19711 
— 
- . we were able to report that lie was subject to long lapses. albeit with 
“ flashes of his old b ril­ liance. 
We also described him as "ail emaciated invalid

This was a terrible secret which the 
Hughes empire labored m ightily to dispel.

AUSTIN — Comptroller Bob 
Bullock raised his revenue estimate for the next two fiscal years Monday by $33 million. 
The new estimate was a slight in­ crease over the $2 billion projected thus far. 
He said, however, that if m ajor spending and tax relief b ills now before the legislature pass, there will be a $3o4 million shortfall. 
A big factor in the increase was a 
$6o.4 million jump in the estimate for motor vehicle sales tax receipts. 
He said when the previous estimate was made, there were “ grave doubts" about the ability of the nation's econ- 

B ullock said when the previous estim ate was m a d e, there were gra v e doubts about the ability o f the nation's econom y to avoid a severe recession 

 omy to avoid a severe recession. 
"These doubts have largely been re­ solved on the basis of the slower than expected recovery we experienced during the latter half of 1o76. Our new estimate assumes that fiscal 1o77 will be another relatively good year for motor vehicle sales, and that fiscal 
1o78 and 1o79 will see moderate, steady growth.“ Bullock said. 
A bill to beef up highway financing would dedicate three-fourths of the motor vehicle sales tax to road con­ struction. Bullock said his revised es-

State Depository Board on Jan. 14 cut the interest rate from seven to six per cent. 
Without comment, he subtracted from estimated revenue, the Legisla­ tive Budget Board’s recommended general appropriation bill. the high­ way bill, the governor's school finance bill, repeal of the sales tax on residen­ tial natural gas and electricity bills, inheritance tax relief and certain emergency appropriations. 
The result, he said, was a deficit of 
$3o4 million, and that did not include a teacher pay raise, property tax reform, a sales tax reduction or “ hundreds of other minor proposals."

Communities Organized for 
Public Sen ice brought in boxes containing at least 1o,ooo new and completed registration cards. 
COPS President Beatrice Oallego — who headed the group bringing in th*' cards to Bexar County’s voter regis­ tration office — took the opportunity to again lash out at Mayor Lila Cockrell for rushing ttic* Coastal States (las 
Corp. settlement. 
COPS bad called a press conference 
Saturday to air charges against the mayor over the gas issue. 
Registrations 
COPS produced more than I ll.(Mitt re­ gistrations in only two weeks of effort, according to Mrs. Oallego. Bexar 
County Voter Registration Off ice Mgr. 
Boger Berry confimcd the organiza­ tion’s effort has made a difference: 
“ Registration was really slow until 
COPS got involved.” he said. So far. 
Berry has not had a chance to examine the boxes of registration cards brought in by C O PS. But voter list totals through Fob. I show 3x2.216 persons are registered to vote. About 12.ooo more have come in since the beginning of the month, the bulk of them through 
COPS, he said. 
That figure amounts to 6X per cent, a record high, of the 556,oo7 voting age jMTsons in Bexar County. Berry noted. 
The figures he deals with are for the entire county, not just the city of San 
Antonio although most of the recent registration activity is geared to the upcoming April 2 City Council election, 
Berry explained.

Pornography fighters, take heart. You have a champion in the Texas Legislature. 

Stale Rep. Ralph W allace of 
Houston has introduced four bills in the House which would put a crimp in the porn trade. They are: 
• House Bill 126o, which would prohibit the showing of minors ob­ serving or engaging in explicit sex­ ual conduct. 
• 
House Bill 1268, which would prohibit the display of “ harmful materials'* at business establish­ ments where minors are part of the general public. 
House Bill 1267. which would increase the penalties for commer­ cial obscenity. 
• 
House Bill 1266. which would prohibit porn businesses from locating within 2.o6o feet of a church or primary or secondary school. 
Book stores 
Currently, W allace is pushing 
HB126K. which is patterned after a 
Detroit ordinance that ha* been upheld by the courts. 
During a telephone interview 
Monday, a W allace aide said: 
“ Ralph represents the Southeast 
Side of Houston, where there’s a growing number of adult book stores and theaters moving in. 
“ The area also has a lot of churches, and members have ex­ pressed concern about getting the porn trade out of the residential neighborhoods where the merchan­ dise is readily available to children. 
“ Tins bill would help flu that." 
So far, half the Harris County delegation and nine other state representatives (none from Bexar 
County) have agreed to co-sponsor 
IIB12KH. 
“ A few liberals have declined to support the bill because they say it will violate First Amendment rights.” said Wallace’s aide. “ But even they recognize it will be a hard bill to vote against.”

While the House is getting ready t<» attack pornographers, over in Hit* 
Texas Senate, salons are preparing to shoot bigger game — you and me. 
For the fifth straight legislative session, the state's small-loan in­ dustry is trying to push through a bill authorizing an increase in in­ terest rates. 
Consumer advocates have beaten back the finance company and bank lobbyists in previous years. But

Well it had to happen sooner or later. 
First Texas AAM made the Cadet 
Corps optional for students. 
Then the school let women in. 
Now a group of homosexual stu­ dents. called the (Jay Student Ser­ vices Organization, lins demanded that the university grant it un-cam- pus recognition. 
The A&M administration has re­ fused — and the war is on. 
Bobby Nelson, an Austin attorney for the OSSO said Monday that a slut will Im* filed against the school in a Houston federal court this week. 
“ A&M is one of the last hol­ douts,” she said. “ But they’re going to have to learn that gay students have to be treated equal.” 
In their petition for recognition. 
(ISSC) m embers explained they wanted to provide professional counseling for homosexual students and to sponsor a speaker's bureau on homosexuality . lVnal code 

A&M Vice President .loltn Kildus responded bv telling the group: 
“ Homosexual conduct is illegal in 
Texas, and therefore it would be most inappropriate lur a state in­ stitution to officially support a stu­ dent organization which is likely to incite, promote and result in acts contrary to and in violation of the penal code of Texas 
Apparently the matter will have to be settled in court. 
M eanwhile, the two dozen members of the OSSO will continue renting meeting facilities off cam­ pus.

Low blood sugar not certainty 

Dear Dr. Thosteson: I went to a doctor who gave me a six-hour glucose tolerance test to see if blood sugar was part of my problem. The reading showed a fasting blood sugar of 9o. Then it went up to almost 
16o an hour later. It then dropped back to 9o the third hour and down to 8o for the fourth, fifth and sixth hours. 
I was told I had hypoglycemia. But when I showed the chart to another doctor, an internist, he said it was a normal test. Who am I lo believe? Is it possi­ ble that there are two schools of thought on this? Do 
I or do I not have low 

Your 
Health 
Bv George C. 
Thosteson, SLD. 

 blood sugar? — Mrs. 
K B . 
You took a pretty ob­ jective test, meaning the numbers speak for themselves. You either have low blood sugar 
(hypoglycemia) or you don't, in your case I concur with the second doctor who said the test was normal. 
True hypoglycemia has blood sugar levels much lower than SO, which was your reading at the conclusion of the six-hours testing. 
On the other hand ________________________ there are different schools of thought. The diagnosis of low blood sugar is made either too frequently and too hastily or it is made too reluc­ tantly. Ordinarily, one doesn’t associate low blood sugar with hypoglycemia-type symptoms (tiredness, irritability, etc.) unless the final reading reaches (IO or below. I think my booklet on low blood sugar may help you in understanding the problem, lf you’d like a copy, send 35 cents and a stamped, self addressed envelope to me care of this newspaper. 

Caruncle 

Dear Dr. Thosteson: I would like your adv ice about a caruncle on the urethra. — Mrs. M VV. 

A urethral caruncle is a tiny growth or a fold in the membrane at the outlet of the urethra (the urinary passage from the bladder). It may give no symp­ toms. Most usually it causes a deviation of the urine stream. Some may bleed easily, be tender lo the touch, or cause pain on urination. 

Some are treated with application of estrogen cream, others by minor surgery to remove the growth or the membrane itself. It is usually seen in women past 5o. The dev iated urinary stream, as well as the painful urination, can be quite a nuisance. 

Are you having a gall bladder problem? To find out how the goll bladder works and what kinds of trouble to look for. read Dr. 
Thosteson s booklet, “ You and Your Gall Bladder, 
W rite him in care of this newspaper, for a copy, enclosing a long, self-ad­ dressed, stamped envelope and 25 cents. 
The Aces 
On Bridge 
Bi IRA C. CORN IR. 

“ The young man knows the rules, but the old man knows the exceptions.” --O W. 
Holmes 
In today's challenging 

 spade game. declarer knew he had to finesse against 
East's trump king 
Both young and old would do it, but only the experienced would do it right 
Some Norths might have raised to two spades im­ mediately. However, the hand was super-light and the distribution left much to be desired 
After North’s pass and So u th s re-opening, 
North felt he was too good for u mere three spades and he gambled out the game 
West led three rounds of hearts and declarer ruffed with hts trump six Three red suit losers were unavoidable, so all hope lay in Bast s possession of the trump king.

Some time ago there 

 was a television special or a pilot on "The Blue 
Knight" before the series started. My husband said 
George Kennedy played in the first one and I say not. Can you help? —N.G. 

The first "B lu e 
K n ig h t" to appear on television was W illiam 
Holden who played 
Bum per Morgan in a four-hour long special on 
NBC-TV. Later a pilot movie was made which starred George Kennedy and it was that which led to the present series.

Can you tell me the 

 other actresses who were 
In contention for the 
Academy Award in 1954 when Grace K elly won the Best Actress Award for her role in “ The 
Country G irl"? Also, can you tell me where they derived the title for the movie? —D.G. 

The other actresses nominated for the Oscar that year were Dorothy 
Dandridge for "Carmen 
Jones.” Judy Garland for 
“ A Star Is Born .” Au­ drey 
Hepburn for 
"Sabrina” and Jane W y­ man for "T he M agnifi­ cent Obsession." Iii the 
Clifford Odets’ play, from which the movie was adapted, the char­ acter of Georgic is de- scribed as a country girl. 

 the CBS-TV special "The 
Minstrel Man.” set for 
Wednesday at 8 p.m. on 
Channel 5. 
M instrel shows, of course, are the shows in which whites donned black face and presented their versions of black song and dance entertainments. 

Are minstrel shows of­ fensive. one wonders? 
They are, Ross says, “ to some even today. A lot of blacks are uptight out of ignorance because they don't know why even blacks, like Bert W il­ liams put on black faces.” 

The reason was simple. 
In those days, around the turn of the century and on for 3o or 4o years, it wasn’t as easy for blacks to get jobs as enter­ tainers. That's why W il­ liams. for instance, used black make-up through­ out his career as a popular vaudeville star. 
There is music in “ The 
Minstrel Man." but Ted says it is not a musical. 

“ It’s more about the life of a minstrel than it is a minstrel show. I play a role described in the script as a charm ing, lovable con man who would rather make a crooked dime than an honest dollar. I think lie’s more of a flim-flam artist than a con man.” 

Iii one part of the story, the young minstrel man played by Glyn Turman gets a job with a circus, as a Zulu in a cage. 

" I recall as a kid in 
Ohio.” Ted says, “ seeing unio. lea says, seeing shows come through 

TED ROSS 

...busy performer 

 town with black guys in cages, advertised as ‘real 
Africans.” ’ 
Born in Zanesville and raised in Dayton, Ohio. 
Ted won a Tony for his role in "The Wiz." That and a movie. "B in g o 
Long and the Travelling 
All-Stars" brought him offers for series. He had a choice of two "C P O 
Sharkey” or "S iro ta 's 
Court.” and he still feels he chose wisely, although 
"C PO Sharkey" seems destined for a run and 
"Sirota’s 
Court” was cancelled. 

Nevertheless, he is now 
"unem ployed” and is planning to go back to 
New York where, he says, it is possible for a performer to show his wares. "Here, if you’re not working the attitude seems lo be you can't be any good." 

Watch Ted in anything he does, and you'll see how mistaken that atti­ tude is. All is not hope­ less out here, either. 
Odds are he won t get to stay in New York Iona.

PORTLAND. Maine - The federal government said Monday it would sue the state of Maine to recover land for the P a s s a m a q u o d d y and Penobscot 
Indian tribes unless an out-of-court settlement was reached by .lune I. 
“ We have no choice but to proceed.” the Justice Department said in papers entered in Federal District Court here in a suit filed by the two tribes to recover their aboriginal lands. 
Valid claim 
Lawyers for the Interior Department and the 
Justice 
Department, the p ap e rs said, had d eterm in ed the In­ dians had a valid legal claim to 5 mil­ lion acres of forest land in northern 
Maine. As the Indians' guardian, the government is compelled to press the 

Commission okays road improvement 

AUSTIN ( A r t - Tilt- Stale High- way and 
Public 
Transportation 
Commission approved Monday the 1977 
State Highway Safety and Betterment an d F a r m to M a r k e t Road 
I m ­ provement Program. 
The commission said the programs includes projects in 212 counties of the state. 
The program calls for improvement of 4.HK5 miles of highways at an e s ­ timated construction cost of more than 
SHI million. 

 claim u n le ss an oui of court settlement is reached, the Justice D epartm ent's brief said. 
President Carter will soon appoint a special federal m ediator to help the parties reach an amicable settlement for submission to Congress, according to the brief. 
The federal government and the In­ dian tribe's, the court papers said. have r e a c h e d an a g r e e m e n t to hold in abeyance the fate of 2 million acres of land. 
That land, which is along the coast and is the most populous and valuable land in the claim area, will be left out of the suit if either th e sta te or the federal government passes legislation allowing the I n d ia n s to sue for the monetary value of the land and fur the 

 dam ages of illegal occupation, accord­ ing to th e a g r e e m e n t r a t i f i e d at a meeting of the tribes Monday. 
Agreed 
Tile Indians also ag reed , the brief said, not to p u r s u e land or d a m a g e c l a i m s a g a i n s t a n y h o m e o w n e r o r s m a l l p r o p e r t y h o l d e r in t h e now-modified claim area if they can instead sue either the state or federal government for the monetary value of the claims. lf no agreement is reached, the J u s ­ tice Department said, it will file test lawsuits against “ a limited num ber of major landowners” — presumably the giant timber and paper companies that are the most important forces in Hun state's economy and politics.

Intercut rate ceiling expires 

WASHINGTON —- The'ceiling on the interest rates which savings and loan institutions can offer savers expired at midnight, but industry officials said not to expect any bargains. 
This will be the fourth such lapse in seven years. In the previous three instances, there were no attem pts to offer savers higher than mandated rates.

SAN ANTONIO EXPRESS— Tuesday March I I 977 

S. A. Ballet’s 'Giselle’ proves outstanding 

B A LLE R IN A Leanna V id a u rri p e rfo rm s d u rin g the San A n ­ tonio B a lle t C o m p a n y 's production of “ G ise lle .” 
Vocal recital due from Miss Cariama 

A vocal recital cover­ ing selections from lite 
ITIKI* to the 2otli Century will be presented by 
Marvellee Cariama at 3 p.m. Tuesday in the 
McAllister Fine Arts Au- 
Art class would aid viewers 

A class beginning 
Tuesday ut the Universi­ ty of Texas at Sun An­ tonio will give people an opportunity to know w hat it is they like when view­ ing a piece of art. 
The UTSA division of 
Continuing Education 
Courses is present­ ing a class entitled “ Art 
Appreciation: Viewing a 
Work of Art.” 
Judith ll. Urrutia. who teaches drawing, paint­ ing and art history at St. 
Phillip's College, will in­ struct the course which w ill be bel*) in the 
Lutcher tenter. (LU) Ivv 
Lane. 
The class meets each 
Tuesday from 7:3(1 to 9:3o p.m. for four weeks. 

 ditorium. 
The mezzo-soprano's recital is being sponsored by the Tuesday Musical 
Club. 
A member of the San 
Diego Opera since 197o. 
Miss Cariagu has sung IO leading roles with that company. 
She won national pro­ minence in 1972 when she stepped in on 24 hours notice for the ailing Irene 

Dalis in the world pre­ miere performance of 
Alva Henderson's “ Me­ dea” in San Diego. 
Miss Cariaga s Tues­ day performance will in­ clude works bv George 
F rid e ric 
Handel. 
Wolfgang Mot/art and 
Samuel Barber.

Presley due iii Austin 

SPECIAL IO THE EXPRESS 
AUSTIN — Tickets for a concert by Elvis Pres­ ley March 28 in Austin go on sail* Tuesday in this city 
The tickets will be sold 
8 a.m.-6 p.m. Tuesday at the Austin Municipal Au­ 

 ditorium box office. 
After Tuesday the tick­ ets will be on stile IO a in -6 p.m. daily. 
The auditorium is the only location the tickets will be sold. Prices are 
812.73 and SI3.23. 

By JOSIE NEAL 
The Sun Antonio Baltei 
Company gave an out­ standing performance in 
“ G is e lle " 
Sunday afternoon at Incarnate 
Word College Auditor­ ium. 
Giselle is a ballet which challenges even the most experienced companies and San An­ tonio Ballet proved itself equal to the task. 
Much of the credit for this achievement goes to 
Leanna 
V id au rri. a 
17-year-old dancer. whose portrayal of the title role was beautiful. rids role requires consi­ derable acting ability, as well us technical vir- tuousity. and is usually given to only the most seasoned ballerinas. 
Mis* Vidaurri brought youth and freshness to the part and danced with perception and sensitive tv. 
In her portrayal of the young peasant girl. dri­ ven mad by the betrayal of the faithless Albrecht. she seemed fragile and vulnerable, yet the sus­ tained quality of her movements showed remarkable strength and control. 
The restraint with which she handled the mad scene gave it a vali­ dity often lacking in the performance of more mature dancers. 
Promise 
Richard Prew itt, us 
Albrecht, showed himsel to be a young man OI considerable promise. He achieved stunning eleva­ tion in his leaps, and executed tours and entrechats with impres­ sive' precision. 

Review 

As att actor, however. 
Ins presence was not commanding: one wished that he would engage the audience with his eyes and establish some com­ munication of his char­ acter. 
The Act I Pas de Deux was charmingly danced by Terry Hayes and Jo ­ shua Thompson. Miss 
Haves has a clean line and a lyrical ease which 

 were also evident in her second act portrayal of one of the leaders of the 
Wilts. 
Cynthia Wood brought to the role of M yrthe. queen of the Wills. a fe­ minine quality that was a pleasant surprise, as this role is often given a cold, harsh interpretation. 
Miss Wood has an ele­ gant line and dances with authority. 
Hesitant 
Robert Reyes, as Hi- larion. the rejected sui­ tor. gave a convincing 

 performance, bul his dancing was somewhat hesitant. 
The corps de ballet showed good ensemble, and their port cie bras were soft and lovely, although some lacked disicipline and polish in their technique. There were a number of young dancers of evident talent. and their further deve­ lopment bear* watching.

Council gives time for gas deal help 

Three or four key city council m em b ers are going the extra mile in attempting to give c ri­ tics of a proposed Coastal States 
Gas Corp. settlem en t tim e to point out im p ro v e m e n ts or killing flaws. 
The delay is to show council understanding of a problem that everyone already understands: 
The p rice of n atu ral g as has gone too high too fast for many of us to adjust without hardship. 
City council is under intense pressure, some of it well moti­ vated. to try for a better settle­ ment but the apparent fact is that n eg o tiato rs have met council conditions for an accep­ table bottom -line figu re. The pressure to force the governor or the Railroad Commission to in terven e has p ersu ad ed the mayor to try for such interven­ tion or. at least, interest. 
But their intervention or in­ terest follows a path not directly related to the proposed settle­ ment. The price of gas on the 
Texas market is a m atter for the market or the Legislature. It is beyond the reach of negotiators in the pending dispute. 
San Antonio holds a crucial role in the negotiations. Coastal 
States' -UMI customers, of which the city is the largest, were said 

 to be ready to take a settlement last July. San Antonio turned it down. That move, championed by this newspaper, turned out to be a good one because the plan was improved materially. 
This time, the improved plan will almost have to be approved by San Antonio before others sign to accept. Best information cu rre n tly is that other customers are waiting to see what San Antonio does. 

Council m em bers have given the fullest kind of examination to the p rop osal, including a public hearing on it. Even so, until all the term s outlined in the package are met and a court order signed, the settlement is still open. 

Council's Monday delay on an informal vote on the proposal was another show of concern for the problem of meeting utility bills. It appears to us that m ax­ imum attention is being given to any possibility of rate relief, but the fact is that the rate problem is nationwide. 
We think council is being re­ sponsible and extremely patient in seeking fullest possible bene­ fits for citizens. Political pres­ sure is obviously making a few m embers groggy.

Firefighters, given extensive e m e rg e n c y m ed ical tra in in g , m an the m o d u lan ces that r e ­ spond to emergencies anywhere iii the county in a m atter of a few minutes.

(‘quipped to give life-saving ser- 
\ ice in many instances. 
Anyone who has seen the crews in action can only be baf­ fled that it to o k so long for us to eliminate the old grab-and-run ambulance techniques of just a few short years ago. 
Now the tim e has co m e to train a new class of Em ergen cy 
M edical 
T e c h n ic ia n s .

4o to give the Fire Department enough budget to pay overtime when needed to staff places left open by new EM T trainees go­ ing to class. 
T im in g is hard on budget- making, but the request should be taken seriously. 
The em otional d rain on 
EM Ts, the doctors say, requires rotation to keep crews in good shape. Revenue Sharing money would be eligible for EMS duty and there is about $3.5 million yet to be earm arked. EMS has proved its case and deserves top priority funding to keep its qua­ lity high.

Sir: Some of my happiest memories as I grew up involved my activities in the Girl Scouts. 
I was shocked that this wonderful organization would come out in favor of the Equal Rights Amendment. 
How elated Betty Friedan must have been the day she was appointed to the national board. 
A made-to-order organization with hundreds of thousands of young girls and women who could further the goals of the women’s liberation movement. 
It took her, and those of her human­ ist beliefs, a little over two years to turn the organization into a political arm of the lib movement, and pervert the original purposes of the G irl 
Scouts. 
I think you're going to see parents such as myself not willing to have their daughters used as political pawns. 
Mrs. Sara Partridge 

Detoxification unit supported 

Sir: A man has just been found dead in his cell at the Bexar County Ja il, apparently from blows to the abdomen. 
According to news reports, the man was picked up on a public drunkeness charge and resisting arrest. 

This death could probably have been prevented if our community, instead of arresting obviously intoxicated p e r­ sons. provided a detoxification service. 
At any given time. the local jail houses at least 2oo such persons. Some are provided with counseling, but appar­ ently not enough to make our streets safer. 

Every arresting officer knows that not every person who staggers, slurs his speech, etc., is under the influence of alcohol. It is unfair to expect him to diagnose every case. 

A diabetic may be in a life-and-death peril; likewise a person with a mental or other physical disorder. I know a man suffering from chorea who has been arrested in another city over UMI times for drunkeness while he is ac­ tually slowly dying from this disorder. 
The persons undergoing m ed ical treatment or those* under the influence of illegal drugs come under this c a ­ tegory. too. This includes many veter­ ans. Persons with mental problems, violent and non-violent, are in a most disadvantageous position in this city. 
If private tra n sp o rta tio n is not available, the only means of assistance is to call the Police Department, have them jailed , aud then e v e n tu a lly , 
(usually days later), transported to an appropriate facility. 
This, in cred ibly, in clu d es o u tp a­ tients of the Audie Murphy Veterans 
Hospital and the San Antonio State 
Hospital. 
A facility is needed where ill persons a re d ia g n o sed . San A ntonio is a medically oriented metropolis. Let us not ignore the least attractive cases, and continue to put off the establish­ ment of a pre-detention detoxification unit. 
Mrs. Minnie Loera

It s great plan for a new tax 

Sir: Martin (inland. Southwest R e­ search Institute, told a group of en­ gineers of an Environmental Protec­ tion Agency proposal to automobile manufacturers which is unsettling. 

According to Goland, American au­ tomobile m akers have decided they can't meet E P A standards for 1978 so they will go ahead with production of cars which do not meet the standards and see what happens. This seem s to he a gam ble that they have every right to take. 

But the E PA . according to the SwRI engineer, at least intimated to the ear makers that it was all right for them to make automobiles which did not meet the standards. E PA would then levy a fine on each car and the cost of the fine could be passed on to the consumer. At least this is the way I understood the 
SwRI official as he appeared on local telev ision news. 

I hope either he was in error, or that 
I misunderstood him. Assuming he was correct, what a beautiful way to lay on an additional tax. Every buyer of a new 1978 ear (or perhaps it would not be felt until 1979) would find himself paying say an extra $15o or 
$2oo in the sticker price. 

Included in that price is a predeter­ mined amount which will go or has already gone to a federal agency in the form of a fine. All fines go directly into the U.S. Treasury, and therefore be­ come general revenue. 

George Ill's chancellor of the exche­ quer is surely turning over in his grave for not thinking up such a gim m ick for below-standard tea leav es. If he had. the pesky Colonists would have had nothing to revolt against. 
Thomas N. Thompson

Carter gives support to U.N. 

W ASHINGTON - In the past few days, President ( arter has had a new grandson, donated blood to the Red Cross and advocated a number of good but neglected causes. 
T h e se include marriage, courtesy, punctuality, clarity, brevity and the 
United Nations. 
This, of course, was in addition to dealing with m ajor questions like the budget; taxes; the Canadian. Mexican and Panamanian problems, and the impending struggle with Japan over fishing rights in the new 2oo-mile 
/one off American shores. 

But with < arter, the little things, the sudden moves, and the side re­ m arks are oiten significant and so­ metim es amusing 
Rich and poor 
During the d e i (ion campaign, he said , “ T h e tim e has com e to stop taxing poor people iii ra h count ne* for the benefit of ru b people in poor countries 
Nobody paid much attention 

It sounded like one uf those sos! mg 
(I it sen from the 

Kennedy days, hut President Carter cut hack on foreign aid to Argentina, 
Uruguay and other cou n tries that have been violating his notion of hu­ man rights. 
He didn’t knock hack aid to South 
K orea or th e P h ilip p in e s , w hose commitment to human rights is not excessive. 
Consistency is not C a r te r’s most prominent characteristic, 
He uses principle with the utmost sincerity — until it conflicts with the national strategic interest. 

In short, he is a superb politician, neither as virtuous as he sounds nor as cynical as he sometimes appears. 

Fast answers 
Lately he has been telling his White 
House staff and ail other employes of the federal government to answer all official telephone calls within three davs and all official m ail within a week 
And at the same time. ne has been telling them to get hom e on tim e every night, enjoy the comforts and co ns o la tio n s of family life and come tack to work rested in the morning 

This is one of these am iable Carter 

 contradictions between two desirable hut conflicting ideals. 
For it can ta staled as an unavoid­ able law of life in Washington that you can cither answer your telephone calls and mail on time or get home on time, hut you can't do both. 
And then are other difficulties. 
Plenty of work 
Most of the new Carter team h a s plenty of phone calls and mail. hut no homes to go home to. 

Some hav e f a m i l i e s down iii Atlan­ ta and ca n 't afford to rent or buy taluses in W ashington 

Some like Cy Vance have rented the old F e lix F r a n k f u r te r aud Henry 
Kissinger house they seldom see 

Some in the White House are luck­ ier in politics than in love, and have 

 children hut no wives, and don't know where home is 
But Jim m y keeps advocating the ideal. 
His attitude 
Ills attitu d e toward the fed eral b u r e a u c r a c y in W a sh in g to n and toward the United Nations illustrates the point. 
He is knocking off a multitude of 
‘’advisory c o m m itte e s .” whose e x ­ penses are m ore visible than their adv ice. 
He is appealing personally to the employ ex of the big federal govern­ ment departm ents, who have never seen a President in their q uarters before, to cut the pa|M*r work and ta* brief, punctual aud precise in their communication* with each other and with the public. 
And he is Irving th restore public confidence in the United Nations. 
Accordingly. Presidents Kennedy. 
Johnson. Nixon and Ford. and even 
Henry Kissinger, while agreeing with the principles of the U N. Charter, gave up on the United Nations as a practical instrument for world order, aud the Israelis ca m e to regard it even as a m en ace to peace in the 
Middle East aud the world 

But President Carter has not taken that view, He sent his friend Andrew 
Young to the United Nations as his ambassador, and is really listening to 
Young in the Cabinet. 
And he brought Secretary General 
Waldheim to Washington to d ram a ­ tize 
Ins confidence in the 
Unit­ ed Nations and its Charter. 
These sym bolic acts by C arter — the insistence on austerity, punctua­ lity, on courtesy aud respect for the ideals of the United Nations — tend to puzzle W ashington, for it has been disappointed in its ideals for so long. hut Carter now has this town working and hoping and even believing again. 
Maybe his White House aides are not going to answer their I cit* phones or m ail on tim e and get hom e oil time. even if they finally find houses they can afford to get home to 
— which is not likely — fur ( arter himself is working IM hours a day. 
And lits aides are obi musty going to follow his model. 
C a rter is in sistin g al hom e and abroad that you can get everybody in on the act. aud still get some action 
It is some challenge, bul be is ob­ viously determined to prove that lie's right 

National press vs. national security 

WASHINGTON 
— 
Do the 
A m e rica n people h ave “ the right to know” the identity of the foreign informants of the 
Central Intelligently Agency? 
When the skyline of The Washington 
Post. Feb. 18. bannered a story about 
2o y e a r s of CIA p a y m e n ts to K ing 
Hussein of Jord an, it answered “ y e s." 
Evidently, the President disagrees. 
At last week's news conference, he noted pointedly. 
“ It can he extrem ely dam aging to our relationships with other nations, to the potential security of our country even in peacetime, for these kinds of operations, which are legitim ate and proper, to he revealed." 
Carter right 
President Carter was right; and The 
Washington Post is dead wr:>ng. 
So, too, were the nionkey-see. inon- key-do pack journalists who, trotting as usual after the Post, rooted out the nam es of other foreign officials who have cooperated with, and received payments from, the CIA. 
Those payments to the king of J o r ­ dan have been known to at least six presidents, twice as many secretaries of state and CIA chiefs, scores of other officials, elected and appointed. R e ­ publican and Democrat, charged with protecting the security of the United 
States. 
Individually and collectively , they decided to keep this m atter secret for reasons of national security. 
Who elected editor? 
Who in the devil elected the editor of 
The Washington Post to overrule that judgment? 
If the people have “ th e rig h t to know” the overseas informants of the 
CIA. do we also have “ the right to know” the nam es of paid informants of the F B I who have infiltrated organized crim e? 
Should th e ir n a m e s , too, be pub­ lished upon discovery, as well as the name, rank and num ber of every So­ viet m ilitary officer so foolish as to cooperate clandestinely with the Unit­ ed States? 
We a r e not ta lk in g h e re about 
“ a b u s e s ” su ch as th e 
K ennedy brothers’ plots to use Mafia thugs to blow away Castro, or brother Bobby's hugging — for high national security purposes — of the bedroom of Martin 
I.ut her King. 
What is involved, as the President has stressed, is the legitimate; gather­ ing of foreign intelligence. 

Restrictions 
Unless one argues that the CIA must ta* restricted to clipping Middle East newspapers, how else, other than using informants, paid and unpaid, can we acquire the inform ation upon which may depend the lives of thousands of 
Arabs and Israelis? 
The king of Jordan has been a public friend and private ally of the United 
States for almost 25 years, a force for moderation in the Arab world. 
Now. thanks to the unilateral action of The Washington Post, he has been compromised and damaged — to the delight of the radical Palestinians. 
One wonders if there is any leader left iii the third world not now co n ­ vinced of the utter insanity of cooper­ ating with intelligence officers of the 
United States. 
Oath violation lf a CIA official leaked this story he violated his oath, and should ta* fired in disgrace. 
But if it be dishonorable for govern­ ment officials to ta*tray their com m it­ ments and their country’s secrets, are we supposed to hail the journalists who publish and enrich them selves upon the fruits of such acts of dishonor and treachery? 
The President has been altogether too kind in his criticism . 
Tru e, the CIA has overstepped its hounds in the past. 
Bul that (hies not give the national press the right to expose national se­ crets — as a matter of their own dis­ cretion. 
True, many officials have abused the 
“ C lassified ” stam p to cover up bur­ eaucratic blunders. 
But that does not g iv e the p ress corps carle blanche to declassify and publish any secret that falls into its hands. 
Walter C'ronkite 
Som e y ears ago W altei C ronkite. speaking for his profession, grandly declared: 
“ I don’t think it is any of our business what the moral, political, so­ cial or economic effect of our retorting tx.” 
Because the national press has often follow cit that arrogant dictum, it has become further estran g ed from the people it purport! to serve 
In setting itself above (be congres­ sional oversight com m ittees, the CIA, the National Security Council and the 
President — in deciding which of the nation's vital secrets to expose — the 
Imperial Press is setting itself up (or a hard fall 
The ms mer it comes* I t a better tor the country 

Space gives a chance to see unknown 

SAN ANTONIO EXPRESS— Tuesday Morch I 1977 
Page J I-A 

Bv ISAAC ASIMOV 
COPYRIGHT 1 9 7 6 H U O t*JTt*l»etSES. INC 
Investigations into th e un­ known, with the resultant broadening of human know­ ledge, can be carried on in space to an extent that might be dif­ ficult or im possible on the earth’s surface. 
, In the previous sect ion. I discussed the possibility of industrial advances in space •- that every one of the properties of space that m akes some in d u strial operation po ssib le also makes a direction of research possible. 
For instance, vacuum distillation, which makes it possible to separate biological substances in great purity, might also help us to understand more about their structure and function. 
The use of hard vacuum, high and low temperatures, energetic radiation 
— all these may give us new tools for studying the structure of materials, for experimenting with the kind of plasma needed to make nuclear fusion p rac­ tical. for studying quantum effects at low temperatures. 
Then, too. there is the m a tte r of weightlessness and its possible effect on li\ mg creatures. 

Complex system 

Generally, when one wants to un­ derstand the workings of a complex system that is subjected to many dif­ ferent conditions, one p roceeds by varying one condition at a tim e and studying the results. 
But one condition we can't easily vary is the gravitational pull of earth 
Only in space can we experience low gravity, for extended periods of time. 
Astronauts have been subjected to zero g rav ity for as long as th re e months and have not experienced any permanent ill effects; nor have they found it im possible to do hard and demanding work. 
There is one concern, though: A slow but steady loss of calcium from the

Berg retraced the long history of the dispute and m ade the motion to ap ­ prove. 
Voting with him were Mayor Lila 
Cockrell and Glenn Biggs. 
Eloy Centeno, the vice chairm an. voted against approval. Only last week he published full-page newspaper ad­ vertisements urging citizens to contact council m em bers and urge disappro­ val 
Phones 

And telephones were ringing off the hook at City Hall Monday from people opposing the settlement. 
Council secretary Barbie Hernandez 

Before earth! nlk can he at borne in a space settlem ent, we must answer some vital questions. 

What effect does zero gravity have on childbirth or on surgical procedures? 

What are the chances of our catching an alien and lethal germ against which we have no defense? 

In this third of a four-part series, popular science-flction author Dr. 
Isaac Asimov spells out the sweep­ ing im plications of space for medical and scientific research. 

 bones, which doesn’t seem to stabilize. 
This must be taken care of if human beings are to become completely ut home in zero gravity. 
Under what conditions can this be arrested or reversed? 
Is the one-sixth normal gravitational pull on the moon’s surface enough? 
Would it be sufficient to place a space station under spin to produce a centrifugal effect that would imitate gravity? 
Would a periodic return to reasona­ bly high gravity help? 
Nor arc such investigations just a m atter of trying to extend the tim e during which people may rem ain at zero gravity. 
By experimenting in this m anner. we may learn something about how calcium is deposited in the bones and how it is withdrawn. 
Many aspects 

In fact. we may find it possible to study many aspects of life at different levels of gravity. 
What happens if animals (and even­ tually human beings), are allowed to give birth at low, or even zero, gravity? 
What happens to young animals (and eventually human beings) who are al­ lowed to grow and develop under low. 

 or even zero, grav tty? 
Will there be changes? 
Can the young adapt? 
What will this tell us about life? 
As an outgrowth of this, w ill there be medical treatm ents that can be con­ ducted more efficiently at low grav ity? 
Any operations? 
Will future generations see not only scientific research stations in space, but specialized hospitals as well? 
Heart patients, for example, could be treated with relative ease once hospi­ talized; but getting them there poses a problem because of a c c e le ra tio n , which works like gravity. 
It would be as though the patient were weighed down by twice the nor­ mal gravity while en route from earth. 

Vast distances 

An advantage of space is that its vast distances can insulate the earth from dangerous experiments. 
Biologists are learning, for instance, how to take genes apart and then put them together again in different combinations. 
In so doing, there is the possibility they will create new varieties of germ s with the potential to illum inate the inner details of life or to p rodu ce chemicals of types that would be useful to us. 
The danger is, however, that, with­ out meaning to. biologists may create germs which are capable of producing diseases against which human beings have no defense. 
There is the frightening possibility of a new and more deadly Black Death sweeping the world. 
The chances of that are very tiny; but in such matters, who wants lo take even a tiny chance? 

Strong feelings 

T here a re , as a re s u lt, stro n g feelings about stopping this sort of re­ search — which would be a sh am e because it might well bring us a great 

GIANT TELESCOPES on space platforms, free from the earth’s atmosphere, could give m an more know ledge about the secrets of the universe. 

 deal of useful information and products. 

But. in that case. why not set up such experiments in space stations, many thousands of miles away? 

The dangers would then be confined, for the most part, to the scientists and technicians aboard the station 

They would know the character of those dangers and would be able to assess the risks. 
The chance of infecting earth would not be zero. since people and objects would travel from station to earth and back again; but, c e r ta in ly , those chances would be much smaller than if the search were being done right here on our planet’s surface. 

Too dangerous 

There may be many other types of research which, for one reason or an­ other. are too dangerous to be carried out here and must be carried out in space laboratories instead. 
Iii such situations any benefits der­ ived from research will be available to all hum anity; any d a n g e rs will be confined to the experimenters. 
Of course, the m ost obviou s ad van ­ tage to research iii space lies with astronom ers. 
A stronom y is the oldest of the sciences and. for many thousands of years, human beings have been study­ ing the sky w ondering about the 

 universe and our place in it 
How large is it? 
How old is it? 
Where did it come fromo 
W here is it going? 
What's happening in it? 
Better instruments 
Steadily, scientists have improved their instruments, dev ising ever larger and better telescopes, cam eras, spec­ troscopes, microwave receivers. 
Always, though, they have had to watch the heavens from the bottom of an ocean of air which stopped many of the kinds of radiation that would oth­ erwise reach us and contribute to our knowledge of the universe. 
Even the ra d ia tio n th a t p a sse s through the atmosphere is twisted out of its path and partly absorbed. 
But we arc beginning to move outward. 
We can send satellites beyond the atm osphere to record and tra n sm it data: the astronauts on Skylab did as­ tronomical work of great value. 
Go farther 
What if we go farther? 
What if we launch a large telescope 

 i i i space — on an orbiting laboratory that could be used and maintained by human beings? 
It would provide a view of the far reaches of the universe without any atmospheric interference. 
It would detect the whole range of radiation; there could be added special 

 in stru m en ts for the d etectio n and study of cosmic rays, g a m m a rays, radio waves and more. 
Such a large space telescope could discern stars in the inner regions of large globular clusters, thus telling us a great deal more than we know now about the evolutions of stars. 
It could show- us fainter and m ore distant galaxies and give us a better idea of the over-all structu re of the universe — perhaps making it possible for us to choose from among the dif­ ferent theories of the fate of the un­ iverse now being offered. 
We m ight he able to decide, say, whether the universe will expand for­ ever, or whether it will gradually stop expanding and begin to contract again. 
Quasar secrets 
What we learn through a large space telescope may tell us finally w hat quasars are — those m ysterious ob­ jects that seem much smaller than or­ dinary galaxies, yet radiated hundred times as much energy as an ordinary galaxy does. 
It may tell us more about mysterious black holes, the graveyards of m atter. even the doom of the universe. 
P erhap s we will learn enough to discover forces and processes at work iii the universe of which we are now- ignorant, but which someday may be bent to the good of humanity.

Texas warming up 

ASSOCIATED PRESS 
Brisk temperatures which greeted Texans Mon­ day morning were replaced by mild temperatures under fair skies later in the day. A warming trend was forecast for the next several.days. 
Humidities were low across the entire state Mon­ day and most areas experienced clear skies and bright sunshine, though a few clouds dotted the skies of the eastern and southern portions of the state. 
Winds from the southwest reached 15 miles per hour in the Panhandle, hut light and variable winds prevailed in other regions of Texas. 
Temperatures ranged from .‘18 at El Paso to 65 at 
Brownsville, with temperatures in the forties and fifties in Central Texas. 
Clear to partly cloudy skies and southerly winds are forecast for early this week, bringing a warming trend The outlook for midweek calls for more clouds and a possibility of rain. 

W ASH IN G TO N (A P ) 
— The Supreme Court said 
Monday it w ill decide whether states have the power to limit the size of oil tankers us­ ing their ports. 
A large oil company arid a shipping firm — backed up by a federal court in Seattle — say such laws encroach on federal statutes and are undue burdens on inter­ state commerce 
Thji state of Washing- toTTTjoined by IO other state's, argues that it has a right to protect its na­ tural resources against oil spills. 
The justices agreed to review a ruling by a three-judge panel that the Washington law must yield to a less restrictive federal law. 
Atlantic Richfield Co., which owns and operates an oil refinery at Cherry 
Point. Wash., on Puget 
Sound, and 
Seatrain 
Lines, a ship building and shipoperating firm, successfully challenged a state law passed in 1975. 
The law contains three major provisions: 
—All “ supertankers." 

 those carrying more than 
125.ooo deadweight ions. are banned from Puget 
Sound. 

—All tankers between 
4o.ooo and 125.ooo dead­ weight tons have to be accompanied by a tug­ boat escort when traveling through the sound or must be equipped with special safety features such as 

 double bottoms under­ neath all stored oil. 
—Tankers over 5o.ooo deadweight tons must have a statelicensed pilot on hoard while the boat navigates the sound. 
The lower court's ruling struck down the law last November but state officials won per­ mission to continue en­ forcing it pending a Su­ preme Court decision. 

The court won t hear ar­ guments in the case until next fall at the earliest giving the state a lengthy reprieve. 
.Joining Washington iii urging the court to allow states to regulate tanker traffic were Maryland, 
Delaware, 
Maine. 
Minnesota. New York. 
California. 
Alaska, 
Pennsylvania. Wisconsin and Missouri. 
Mardian reinstated by Supreme Court 

W A SH IN G TO N ( A P ) - Robert 
Mardian, free of legal entanglement in 
Watergate, again enjoys the right to practice law but seven other attorneys who were linked to the political scan­ dal, including former President R i­ chard M. Nixon, remain barred from such work. 
Mardian. who was an assistant at­ torney general in Nixon's administra­ tion before becoming an official of the 
Committee for the Re-Election of the 
President in 1972, was reinstated as a member of the Supreme Court bar on 
Monday. 

“ Pm delighted,” Mardian said from an office at his fam ily's Phoenix construction company. Although he termed his post-Watergate experience a “ tough four years,” Mardian, 53. said he is not bitter. 

Asked whether he plans to reenter politics, Mardian said, “ I hadn’t even thought of that. I figure I ve given my pound of flesh to the public.” 

Low,er courts already had granted 
Mardian, a 53-year-old Phoenix re­ sident, permission to resume his legal career.

W eather blamed for deficit 

 r:DOW JO N E S IN D U S TR IA L AV ER AGE 
SAN AN TO N IO EXPRESS— Tuesday March I 1977 

W A SH IN G TO N (A P ) 
— 
A record month Iv trade deficit and the bitt­ iest drop in the nation s economic indicators in­ dex since the 1974-75 recession were blamed by the government Mon­ day on the severe Ja n ­ uary weather. 
Because of plant shut­ downs and transportation snarls, exports of goods declined 7.8 per cent during the month to give the nation a deficit in its foreign trade of nearly 
SI.7 billion. 
The indicators index that is supposed to fore­ shadow future economic trends was off 12 per cent in January, the big­ gest decline since a 3 per cent drop in January of 
1975 during the depths of the recession 
The 
Commerce 
Department said the weather was a major contributor to both ad­ verse economic reports. 
It said it “ had a. pron­ ounced impact in dimin­ ishing the volume of goods moving out of the country.” 
Layoffs 

Job layoffs were up. and the average number of hours worked in a week was down. 
But the government still is confident that the economy is im proving and predicts that most of the economic ground that 

 is lost during the winter will be made up during the rem ainder of the year. 
The drop in exports was across the board.

Farm prices rise 

COMPILED FROM WIRE SERVICES 

W A SH IN G TO N 
- 
Prices farmers got for raw products rose 2 per cent between Jan 15 and 
Feb. 15. the Agriculture 
Department said Mon­ day. 
The department’s C rop 
Reporting Board said that higher prices for cultic, broiler chickens. hogs. soybeans, vegeta­ bles and cotton accounted for most of the increase. 
Lower prices were re­ ported for corn and milk, which helped offset the increases for other com­ modities. officials said. 
The new report covered a month when severe freeze damage was sported to Florida citrus and vegetable crops, in­ flicted when tem pera­ tures dropped sharply during three days, Jan. 
18-2o. 
The report showed that orange prices paid to growers as of Feb. 15 averaged SH cents a box. 

BUSINESS DIGEST 

 up from 82 cents a month earlier before the freeze. 
A year earlier oranges were $1.93 a box.

Lead price* lip 

N E W 
Y O R K 
- 
A number of major lead producers raised prices 
Monday, a possible after-effect of this win­ ter's exceptionally cold weather. 
The storage battery in­ dustry is a major user of lead, and observers have noted that replacement batt*ry sales rose mar­ kedly during the cold. 
Among producers boosting prices 2 cents a pound to 31 cents were 
NL Industries. Inc.. St. 
Joe Minerals Corp. and 
Gulf Resources & Che­ mical Corp.'s Bunker 
Hill Co. div ision. 

P ro d u c tio n o f steel rises 
N EW YORK - Steel production climbed to 2.3 million net tons in the week ended Feb. 2H. un increase of 2 per cent over the preceding week s 2.28 . million nut tons. tin* American Iron and Steel Institute re­ ported Monday. 
The industry’s produc­ tion amounted to 75 I per cent of its capacity dur­ ing the week, compared with 73.H per cent in the previous week 
Estimated figures for the year to date showed production at 17.2 million net tons. a decrease of 7.5 per cent from the 18 8 million net tons in the previous year. 
Production so far this year used 88 8 per cent of industry eapactiy, com­ pared with 78 5 per cent a year earlier.

■ 1.2 per cent drop in its leading-indicators index during January. 
O rdinarily a drop in the index, which is de­ signed to detect future economic trends, might have raised concern about prospects for busi­ ness activity in the months ahead. 
Garble 
But traders seemed to be going on the assump­ tion that the forecast made by the index in 
January was garbled by shortterm problems posed by severe weather. 
Analysts also noted that the news had been widely anticipated on 
Wall Street. They traced some late buying to ten­ tative hopes that the market might have pret­ ty well finished taking into account the winter weather s adverse effects on the economy.

Gold surges 

LONDON (A P ) - Gold surged to a 15-month high in London on Mon­ day. gaining $3 an ounce to clo s e at $143 in ad­ vance of next Wednes­ day's gold auction in 
Washington. 
In Zurich, Europe's other main bullion center, the metal gained nearly $3 from Friday to close at $142,875. ll was the highest Lon­ don price since Nov. 13, 
1975. when gold closed at 
$143.83.

193os but showed no rush to buy.

Dry weather lets ranchers work land 

Two weeks of dry weather and high winds on several days have made it possible for many land owners to work their land sooner than they had predicted. 
•lust before the start of the San Antonio Lives­ tock Show and Rodeo, many farm ers and ranchers said they would need three weeks to six weeks of dry weather for them to uet their land into condition for plant­ ing. 
The three weeks is inst alinit up now. aud many land owners already have their fields worked over for planting. 
Coastal bermudagrass is lieginning to green up and other grasses are beginning to grow again. 
However, the cold nights have slowed the growth to some extent.

INVESTMENT COURSE 

Conducted by FRANK TINNEY, President, R. I. Stewart $ Co. Inc. 

The course consists of 4 sessions. The first will include a frank discussion of today’s economy, prices, interest rates and the current investment outlook. 

 later sessions will include taxable and tax-exempt bonds, tax-deferred annuities, stocks, funds, and a special discus­ sion on how to make an investment plan to fit your own personal situation. 

Content will be simple, candid and without sales solici­ tation. Each class will include time for questions. The price is a $5 donation (per family) to any charity.

Dimes 

Top Teens have recently returned from an Area I Leadership Involve­ ment Symposium in Tyler. This m eeting was sponsored by Top

Leaders of the TTA are Frank

Heritage 

TTA is two years old in San An­ tonio. One of the special projects for this years was the presentation of a 
Black Heritage program for the 
Senior Citizens at Cartwheel Lodge. 

Each year for Valentine's Day. 
Top Teens supply the County Jail 
Library with magazines, especially ethnic ones, such as Ebony and Jets. 
This is their way of spreading cheer further than family and sweethearts on Valentine’s Day. Magazines and books are collected from the com­ munity. 

At Thanksgiving they furnished food baskets to two families with six to eight members. Baskets were given through Ella Austin Com­ munity Center. Joan Burley handled arrangements for distribution. 

Among the other community Ser­ vices they have undertaken is work with the United Negro College fund-raising project.

Learning to be assertive 

BY JEAN BAER 
Have you ever ordered a rare steak au poivre in a fancy 
French restaurant and had it delivered well done—and short on pepper? 
Have you ever been taken a round­ about way to your destination by an 

 opportunistic taxi driver who. atter you tip him despite his hi-jinks, com­ plains. “ Lady, I ain’t never gonna get rich off people like you!” 
While traveling abroad, have*you ever decided to treat yourself to a weekend at some posh resort and been put in a tiny cubicle across from the cleaning room quarters?

LAW. 

What did you do in those or sim ilar situations? If you’re like many women who lack assertion, you suffered and did nothing. 
To treat people who have these specific problems in assertion. Asser­ tiveness T raining offers a method called behavior assignments. 
The therapist gives out certain tasks— some involving impersonal si­ tuations in stores, restaurants, theaters, supermarkets, and others that concern social interaction with friends, co-workers. husbands, relatives. 
The premise behind these assign­ ments: Knowing what you should do is not enough. The purpose of these as­ signments: to get you to start behaving assertively iii an organized way. 
Get started 
Here are some assertive assignments that may help you to get started on the assertive act and thus you have the knowledge that you can go on to others. 
Use the following assignments as a guide and men tailor-make your own to your individual needs. 
Ask for something in an impersonal situation: 
• In a store where the price list isn t posted, ask to see it. 
• lf you’re driving and aren't sure of the turn-off to your destination, stop at least two people and ask for directions. 
• In the course of the week go into stores and ask for change of one dollar. 
Don’t buy anything. The second week ask for change of $5 and the third for change of 
SIO. 
Again don’t buy anything. 
Part 
> \ 
J? 
• '.vV.. 
$ 
Are You 
. n r r : 
. a Woman or a Mouse? 

The nuiiit here is not to amiss a collection of change but to have the courage to ask. (Note: This assignment is a favorite of behavior therapists who specialize in Assertiveness Training.) 
• Take the classified section of the newspaper and find jobs for which to call. Practice saying. “ I read about your job in the Times. Can you tell me a little about the job and just what it would entail?" 
• If you have difficulty coping in a business situation that is prim arily male-oriented (for example, dealing w ith people Uke auto mechanics), go to a garage and get an estimate of what it would cost to do a repair job on your car, even though you don’t plan to have it fixed. 
Put you first: 
• Take an hour every day to do something for yourself in your own home. It can be something as simple as reading a new best-seller. Repeat this over and over until you are able to do it without feeling guilty. 
• During the week take at least four hours for yourself for “ fun tim e" out­ side of the house. 
• Make the deliberate effort to go to a store and purchase something for yourself—not for a friend, colleague, spouse, children, or mother-in-law. 
• Enroll in a course—typing, karate, finance, or credit toward your M.A. 
• At work tell your boss you need an extra two hours to do a personal errand 
(naturally, this assignment presumes vou do not have the habit of goofing offi. 
This task is particularly good for women who feel a male authority figure will view them as a "female stereotype—flighty and gabby” if they take time for a coffee break or an extra long lunch hour. 
Break a pattern: 
• Do one thing you've procrastinated about for a long time: make an ap­ pointment for a checkup, fix up your personal phone book, w rite your aunt to 

 whom you’ve owed a letter for six mon! hs. 
• Open your own checking account. 
• Sign up for volunteer work requir­ ing responsibility. Or ask for more responsibility on your job. 
Make a social contact: 
• Get in touch with someone you haven’t seen for a long time and make a date. 
• You pick whom you and your hus­ band socialize with for the next three 
Saturday nights. 
• Talk to a stranger. One Westches­ ter housew ife tried this and found both a work project and a close friend. * 
• Make two social overtures in the next week—for instance, invite a cou­ ple whom you have never seen socially over for dinner; suggest to a fellow worker that you lunch together; invite a woman in your exercise class who seems worth knowing to join you for coffee. 
• Ask a man out. lf he refuses, don’t go to pieces. 
• Initiate a contact at a singles bar. 
(Note: This is a favorite behavior as­ signment of therapists, but I must confess it ’s too advanced for me. I couldn't do it.) 
• Try making a personal friend of someone you’d like to know better—and who frightens you. In the course of w riting my book The Second 
Wife, I interviewed Julia Perles, the internationally known lawyer whom 
New York magazine has described as a 
"low-key dynamo." 
Miss Perles not only gave me a two- hour interview but even volunteered to go over the two chapters pertaining to legalities involved in remarriage. I was terribly impressed—by her knowledge and warmth. For months I thought, 
“ I ’d like to be friends with her." Then 
I ’d negate the wish with, “ She wouldn’t be interested in me.” 
One day I summoned my courage and called her. To the secretary I said. 
“ My name is Jean Baer. I interviewed 
Miss Perles for my book, but probably she won’t remember me." 
But Miss Perles did. “ Jean, bow nice to hear from you. How is the book coming?” 
I plunged. "The book w ill be out in 
June, but that s not why I called. I enjoyed meeting you so much. You were nice to me. I wonder if I could take you to lunch.” 
Dead silence. 
I died. "O f course. I realize how busy you are. so 1 11 understand perfectly 
. . 
I trailed off. 
A shock 
Miss Perles interrupted with, “ The shock of being asked to lunch shut me up. You know, in the past year I must have given 4o interviews and not one reporter has even written me a thank­ you note much less asked me for lunch. 
Id love it. 
How about next 
Wednesday?" 
We had a lovely lunch at the Clos 
Normand. As we sipped coffee. Julia (I had progressed to her firs t name) invited me and my husband to a cock­ tail party she was giving that Sunday. 
I hesitated, saying. “ Herb is giving a professional talk somewhere and we wouldn’t be able to get to your house until 7 o'clock." 
She said. "Let him come when he can. You come at five." 
Again. I demurred. “ But do you want a woman alone?" 
She laughed, saying. "W e’ll take cure of you." She and her husband did. 
I have a marvelous time meeting the legal lights of New’ York. The next day three of the party guests telephoned with invitations. 
Julia and I became fast friends—the kind that call each other on Saturday afternoon and say, “ Doing anything tonight? If not. I’ll cook." 

It is such a satisfying aud rewarding friendship, and all because I forced myself to call her when my basic in­ security kept saying. "What would she see in you?"

Annuo! Notional Guard Military Boll in Villita Assembly Building. 
It. Col. McRae is commander of 1st Battalion, I 41st Infantry.

Three San Antonians have been appointed to a 
4o member Texas Coor­ dinating Committee on the rights and responsi­ bilities of women. State 
Representative Sarah 
Weddington of Austin is temporary chairman. 
Local members include 
Lupe Anguiano, founding member of the National 
Chicano Foundation and the National W omen’s 
P o litic a l Caucus, and member of Texas Wo­ men s Political Caucus. 
Jane Haun Macon is treasurer of 
Young 
Lawyers of San Antonio, former chairman of Tex­ as W omen’s P o litic a l 
I aucus; and recorder of 
National Women’s Politi­ cal Caucus. 

Helen Jacobson is on the board of directors of the National Federation of Temple Sisterhoods, chairman of the Com­ mittee on 
Peace and 
World Relations, and a member of the Commis­ sion on Social Action of 
Reform Judaism. 

Internationa) Women’s 
Year Commissioners. Liz 
Carpenter of Austin and 
Gloria Scott of Houston, are ex-officio members of the committee. 
In 
1976, 
Congress named the 
N ational t ommission on the ob­ servance of International 

Women s Year as the sponsor for 56 state meetings (including var­ ious territories), all to be held before July. 1977. 
A National W omen’s 
Conference w ill be held in Houston Nov. 18-21. 
Representatives selected by the state meetings and appointed by the National 
Commission w ill attend the conference. 
An m u rim s te ttin g com m ittee is studying sites and dates for the state m eeting and the fe a s ib ility of regional 

 public hearings. This w ill enable local speuk outs on the status of Texas women. 
Carol Hatfield of Aus­ tin ir- in charge of the site search. Colleges and si­ m ilar facilities as well as commercial facilities are under consideration. In ­ terested bidders and in­ dividuals w ith sugges­ tions may get in touch with her. Slit* is editor of the magazine. Discovery:

Rear Readers: Yester­ day’s column, filled with letters reporting on the shocking behavior of students of all ages, was depressing to say the least. 
It would be unfair to let you twist slowly in the wind without printing a le tte r of explanation, with a suggestion on what m ight be done to im prove the situation. 
Here it is: 
Dear Ann Landers: 
When I read the letter from the Iowa teacher who complained about students being rude, un­ motivated and hostile — and your response (you felt students had shaped up considerably since 
1968 and asked "th e teachers out there" to let you know if you were wrong) — I knew you were in for a real shellacking. 
College students have shaped up and with good reason. First: The Viet­ nam war which tore our country and campuses apart has ended. 
Second: 
M in o ritie s 
(blacks and women) are getting a better break. 
Third: Making a livin g has suddenly become important. 
The economic down­ turn in 197o made college students aware of the stiff competition for jobs. 
The goof-offs decided they had better get with it if they wanted to eat after graduation. 
But the gram m ar school, junior and senior high school students are not concerned with econ­ omics. jobs or anything else. They simply behave at school the way they behave at home — w hieh is to say rotten. 
Women's lib 

Let's face it. Fam ily life is falling apart and 
Women’s Lib has had a lot to do with it. Mothers have been made to feel inferior if they “ do noth­ ing" but stay at borne and take care of their children. 
So the kids are left with incom petent help, or nobody. They grow up ui* secure, hostile, with no models to learn from 
Daddy is divorced or busy working, or there is 
NO daddy and never was. 
A whole generation of young people has grown 

 up with no respect for authority. They do not respect th e ir parents, their grandparents, their neighbors, th e ir teachers, or the law’. (The statistics on juvenile shoplifting and crime are staggering.) the root of the trou­ ble: lack of parental guidance. "As the twig is bent so grows the tree” — and too many twigs are being bent these days in odd and frightening shapes. 
We arc paying for it in the classroom s, in juvenile courts and in our prisons. 
There were over 75.ooo assaults on teachers last year by students. M il­ lions of dollars worth of 

 school property was ruined by vandals and this does not include what was stolen. 
Something has gone haywire in our society and it all goes back to the lack of parental guidance. The solution to the problem lies in the home. I say to every mother and father who reads this column, take 
Ann Landers’ advice. 
She has been te llin g you for over 2o years that discipline is a special kind of love — that kids want guidelines, they not'd to be told how far they can go. They don’t want everything they ask for. 
They often hope you’ll say NO. (It takes them off the hook with their peers). The child w ho can boss his parents and call the shots is miserable and insecure. 
He feels unloved — that nobody cares enough to take charge. What a pity! 
— Spoke My Piece 
Dear Spoke: You cer­ tainly did. and 
I am grateful.

By DAVID HEN DRH KS 
Some 4o citizens made requests 
Monday night at a public hearing un revenue sharing funds — requests totalling far more than the ST.ti million coming to San \ntonio. 
About 4oo citizens, roughly half of. them belonging to Communities Or­ ganized fur Public Service, attended the hearing held by City Mgr. Thomas 
Huebner and staff members 
COPS President Beatrice Gallego and other COPS members along pre­ sented a priority of projects for nearly 
SII million, far over the $7.ti million which will be coming to the city. 
Drainage first 
Number one on COPS list is drainage projects, mainly for streets un the ci­ ty's west and south sides. Mrs. Gallego told Huebner. 
“ We know of no other city in Ameri­ ca's top Kl that has the severity of flooding, neglected streets, drainage and had housing as San Antonio." Mrs. 
Gallego said. 
COPS members also asked fur street improvements, traffic lights and parks lo be funded from general revenue sharing funds. 
Other groups which had representa­ tives deliver appeals for funds Monday night included the San Antonio Boys 
Clubs, the Greater San Antonio Youth 
Symphony Orchestra, the South Texas 
Health Education Center, the Healy- 
Murphy Learning Center, tile Retired 
Senior Volunteer Program and the San 

Antonio Ballet Company. 
Mike Morrow of the North San An­ tonio Chamber of Commerce spoke on behalf of support for an EMS training program. 
$26 m illion asked 

All told. City Operations Mgr. John ll Small reported 1o8 formal requests for funding during the current period total more than $28 million. 
Most of those requests are for one­ time expenditures on c ity -operated projects. Small said. 
The funding period began Jan. I and will end Sept. 39. he added. Huebner said the actual amount to be allocated is less than the $7.8 million because some agencies have been funded since 
Jan. I 
Original intent 
Huebner explained to the audience that the original intent of revenue sharing was for capital improvement 
■projects. 
However, most large cities, includ­ ing San Antonio, have had to use the money for regular operational ser­ vices. he said, blaming the large in­ flation rate. 
“ However, my hope is to allocate as much to capital improvement projects as possible." the told the audience. 
Huebner will make his recommen­ dations to City Council on March lh. 
After another public hearing on April 
6. City Council will approve an alloca­ tion list on April 7.

Northside School District trustees 
Monday hired un internal auditor to continually check individual school accounts. 

Hiring the auditor is a byproduct of an audit last year of the district's three high schools, according to board Vice 
President d a n Philips. 
Trustees Monday also approved a 
$2,ooU increase iii the district's ho­ mestead tax exemption for senior citi­ zens. Senior citizens wanted more. 
The auditor — Calvin Roach — was hired in executive session. Roach is currently comptroller for Gold Bond 
Manufacturing Co in Natalia. 
Even though the auditor was hired as a result of last year's audits. Philips said no missing funds were turned up in the audits 
“We're a big school district and we have a lot of funds to be accounted for." Philips said

During the Monday discussion on the tax exemption for semoj- citizens. G. H 
Sawyer asked the board for a $7,ooo increase. 
That would increase the exemption for Northside senior citizens to $1o,ooo 
— the maximum allowed by law. 
The school district currently has a 
$3,ooo exemption. They approved the 
$-3,ooo exemption for 1977-78. 
“ I'm objecting to the small amount 
I pleaded last year for the full $1o,ooo which the city put into effect in 1974," said Sawyer. He said he was speaking for himself and his neighbors 
The $3.oun exemption costs the dis­ trict $71.ooo per year. A $5,ooo exemp­ tion would cost $112,887 and the $1o,ooo exemption would cost an estimated 
$197,777. according to school district figures. 
In other action .Monday, trustees ap­ proved purchasing lo acres in the 
Timber Ridge subdivision to build the district’s 22nd elementary school. 

Pickets due to stop 

Construction workers picketing the new General 
Postal Facility on Perrm-Beitel Road are expected to return to work Tuesday after a brief stoppage. 
Members of the construction union, Laborers 
International I Ilion l.ocal 93. pic keted the site 
Monday because oi a conflict with one of the subcontractors. < EUO ( opp. 
Members of some craft unions working at the facility refused to cross the picket line and did not work Monday. said Jim Krempen, project manager for the building. 
K rem lin represents the building's general con­ tractor* Hubert, Hunt and Nichols, 
Laltorers Union spokesman Abel Gonzalez said the union and CECO will meet this week to iron out an agreement. 
Gonzalez said 4o members of the union local are without a working agreement with CECO. 
“We will take down the picket line Tuesday,” he said

For nine years we told our­ selves that the merger would be the saviour of pro basketball. It would end the ridiculous bidding war which was bankrupting the sport. 

Now we have a merger but. lo and behold, another big battle of the bucks is iust around the corner, inst a few months away. 
True, the merger will end the outra­ geous salaries being given to the young talent out of college but it will have no control over the free agents. 
Players become free agents by not signing new contracts with their cur­ rent clubs There is no option year involved in NB N contracts. 

When you consider that as many as 
HU players in the NBA are supposedly playing out their contracts this season, you realize the potential bidding war awaiting owners this summer. 

One of those players due to become a free agent in a few months is Pete 
Maravfch of New Orleans. The dollar war for him could easily get out of hand. 

New Orleans, anxious to keep Pistol 
Pete in Cajun Country, might offer 
SSnn.OOft. But the Knicks might reply with a $4oo,ooo offer and then Phila­ delphia just might throw in a bid of 
$5oo,ooo 
Pete will go to the highest bidder. 
Wherever he goes, he will be getting one whale of a raise. 

With so many other players bi‘com­ ing free agents, pro basketball will be facing a critical time in its history lim summer. The future of the sport might depend on the way Com m issioner 
Larry O'Brien handles the free agent cases 

Rozelle Rule 

You see. not much is said about it but pro basketball has the same Ro­ zelle Rule made famous by the NFL. 
That rule, in fact, was ruled illegal in the N F L by at least one judge. 

Let's say Maravieh ends up signing with the Knicks. O'Brien, as commis­ sioner of tin* league, must then order 
New York to give New Orleans some sort of compensation. Maybe one or two players, maybe draft choices or maybe cash 

O'Brien must be careful not to anger the players with extremely still com­ pensation. 

Right now the N BA Players Asso­ ciation and the league have two more years left on their agreement but if the pro eagers become as unhappy with 
O'Brien as the pro gridders were with 
Rozelle. then bad trouble is a certain­ ly. 

N F L players claimed that Rozelle was too harsh iii his compensation rulings. They said that he made the compensation so severe that clubs could not afford to sign free agents. 

In other words, ii the Knicks signed 
Maravieh but were ordered to send 
Bob McAdoo and Malt Frailer to the 
Jazz as compensation, they would think twice belore signing another free agent. 

A r ling that severe would end the bidding war for free agents but it would also result in a ''int by the players association, just as it did iii pro fool hall. 

On the other hand. O'Brien cannot be too lenient, lf compensation is too mild. ll will encourage every decent player in the league to play out Ins contract. 

Since he might have as many as HU cases to deal with this sum m er. 
O'Brien needs to spend a lot of time studying the situation. His decisions must Im* just right — not to strong but nut too weak 

Wasted money 

During the bidding war salaries soared incredibly high and for the league to succeed and grow the payr­ olls must be reduced to realistic fi­ gures. The merger will help but it is not enough. 
The owners are the Lev 

Right now there are many substi­ tutes in the NBA who arc making more than some superstars iii pro football. 
Mack Calvin, for instance, was not helping the Spurs. He was warming the !>ench. although collecting $15o.bon 

(•ail Goodrich doesn't start tor New 
,Orleans bul he makes $3oo,ooo. Young 
Dave Mevers. a sub until receiitlv. makes $3oo,ooo Bill Bradley seldom contributes to the Knicks bul has a 
>285 ami salary, 

This is not to blame the players 
Gracious, if an owner is stupid enough to continue paving such ridiculous sa­ laries. a player would lie super stupid not to accept. 

Elm ore Smith, the sub center at 
Cleveland, reportedly collects $35o IMM) a year when, iii lait. he probably should Im* getting $5o.(MHI 
Such situations are not uncommon hut they must be changed it pro bas­ ketball is to return to firm footing 

Basically, it s up to the owners. They can either continue throwing away money like coconuts, driving each other to financial suicide, or they can start dealing like sound, logical busin­ essman, returning sanity to the s|x>rt.
